Aztec calendar stone.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Humboldt_1810_pp_47_48_50_51_52.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Humboldt_1810_pp_47_48_50_51_52.jpg/220px-Humboldt_1810_pp_47_48_50_51_52.jpg" width="220" height="98" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Humboldt_1810_pp_47_48_50_51_52.jpg/330px-Humboldt_1810_pp_47_48_50_51_52.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Humboldt_1810_pp_47_48_50_51_52.jpg/440px-Humboldt_1810_pp_47_48_50_51_52.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1680" data-file-height="751" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Humboldt_1810_pp_47_48_50_51_52.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Dresden_Codex" title="Dresden Codex">Dresden Codex</a>, later identified as a Maya manuscript, published in part by Humboldt in 1810</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Humboldt and Bonpland had not intended to go to New Spain, but when they were unable to join a voyage to the Pacific, they left Ecuador's port of Guayaquil and headed for Mexico's west coast port of <a href="/wiki/Acapulco" title="Acapulco">Acapulco</a>, landing February 15, 1803.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> Even before Humboldt and Bonpland started on their way to New Spain's <a href="/wiki/Mexico_City" title="Mexico City">capital</a> on Mexico's central plateau, Humboldt realized the captain of the vessel that brought them to Acapulco had reckoned its location incorrectly. Since Acapulco was the main west coast port and the terminus of the <a href="/wiki/Manila_Galleon" class="mw-redirect" title="Manila Galleon">Asian trade</a> from the Spanish Philippines, having accurate maps of its location was extremely important. Humboldt set up his instruments, surveying the deep water bay of Acapulco, to determine its longitude.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDe_Terra1955149-150_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDe_Terra1955149-150-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p>Humboldt and Bonpland arrived in Mexico City, having been officially welcomed via a letter from the king's representative in New Spain, Viceroy Don <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_de_Iturrigaray" title="José de Iturrigaray">José de Iturrigaray</a>. Humboldt was also given a special passport to travel throughout New Spain and letters of introduction to intendants, the highest officials in New Spain's administrative districts (intendancies). This official aid to Humboldt allowed him to have access to crown records, mines, landed estates, canals, and Mexican antiquities from the prehispanic era.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDe_Terra1955156_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDe_Terra1955156-62">[62]</a></sup> Humboldt read the writings of Bishop-elect of the important diocese of Michoacan <a href="/wiki/Manuel_Abad_y_Queipo" title="Manuel Abad y Queipo">Manuel Abad y Queipo</a>, a liberal, that were directed to the crown for the improvement of New Spain.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrading1991527_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrading1991527-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<p>They spent the year in the viceroyalty, traveling to different Mexican cities in the central plateau and the northern mining region. The first journey was from Acapulco to Mexico City, through what is now the Mexican state of <a href="/wiki/Guerrero" title="Guerrero">Guerrero</a>. The route was suitable only for mule train, and all along the way Humboldt took measurements of elevation. When he left Mexico a year later in 1804, from the east coast port of Veracruz, he took a similar set of measures, which resulted in a chart in the <i>Political Essay</i>, the physical plan of Mexico with the dangers of the road from Acapulco to Mexico City, and from Mexico City to Veracruz.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> This visual depiction of elevation was part of Humboldt's general insistence that the data he collected be presented in a way more easily understood than statistical charts. A great deal of his success in gaining a more general readership for his works was his understanding that "anything that has to do with extent or quantity can be represented geometrically. Statistical projections [charts and graphs], which speak to the senses without tiring the intellect have the advantage of bringing attention to a large number of important facts."<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>Humboldt was impressed with Mexico City, which at the time was the largest city in the Americas, and one that could be counted as modern. He declared "no city of the new continent, without even excepting those of the United States, can display such great and solid scientific establishments as the capital of Mexico."<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> He pointed to the <a href="/wiki/Palacio_de_Miner%C3%ADa#History" title="Palacio de Minería">Royal College of Mines</a>, the <a href="/wiki/National_Palace_(Mexico)#Viceroy_palace" title="National Palace (Mexico)">Royal Botanical Garden</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Academy_of_San_Carlos" title="Academy of San Carlos">Royal Academy of San Carlos</a> as exemplars of a metropolitan capital in touch with the latest developments on the continent and insisting on its modernity.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrading1991526-27_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrading1991526-27-67">[67]</a></sup> He also recognized important creole savants in Mexico, including <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Antonio_de_Alzate_y_Ram%C3%ADrez" title="José Antonio de Alzate y Ramírez">José Antonio de Alzate y Ramírez</a>, who died in 1799, just before Humboldt's visit; Miguel Velásquez de León; and <a href="/wiki/Antonio_de_Le%C3%B3n_y_Gama" title="Antonio de León y Gama">Antonio de León y Gama</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrading1991527_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrading1991527-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<p>Humboldt spent time at the Valenciana silver mine in <a href="/wiki/Guanajuato" title="Guanajuato">Guanajuato</a>, central New Spain, at the time the most important in the Spanish empire.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrading1991525_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrading1991525-68">[68]</a></sup> The bicentennial of his visit in Guanajuato was celebrated with a conference at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Guanajuato" class="mw-redirect" title="University of Guanajuato">University of Guanajuato</a>, with Mexican academics highlighting various aspects of his impact on the city.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> Humboldt could have simply examined the geology of the fabulously rich mine, but he took the opportunity to study the entire mining complex as well as analyze mining statistics of its output. His report on silver mining is a major contribution, and considered the strongest and best informed section of his <i>Political Essay</i>. Although Humboldt was himself a trained geologist and mining inspector, he drew on mining experts in Mexico. One was <a href="/wiki/Fausto_Elhuyar" title="Fausto Elhuyar">Fausto Elhuyar</a>, then head of the General Mining Court in Mexico City, who, like Humboldt was trained in Freiberg. Another was <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9s_Manuel_del_Rio" class="mw-redirect" title="Andrés Manuel del Rio">Andrés Manuel del Rio</a>, director of Royal College of Mines, whom Humboldt knew when they were both students in Freiberg.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDe_Terra195551.2C_156_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDe_Terra195551.2C_156-70">[70]</a></sup> The Bourbon monarchs had established the mining court and the college to elevate mining as a profession, since revenues from silver constituted the crown's largest source of income. Humboldt also consulted other German mining experts, who were already in Mexico.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrading1991527_63-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrading1991527-63">[63]</a></sup> While Humboldt was a welcome foreign scientist and mining expert, the Spanish crown had established fertile ground for Humboldt's investigations into mining.</p>
<p>Spanish America's ancient civilizations were a source of interest for Humboldt, who included images of Mexican manuscripts (or codices) and Inca ruins in his richly illustrated <i>Vues des cordillères et monuments des peuples indigènes de l'Amerique</i> (1810–1813), the most experimental of Humboldt's publications, since it does not have "a single ordering principle" but his opinions and contentions based on observation.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> For Humboldt, a key question was the influence of climate on the development of these civilizations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrading1991523_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrading1991523-72">[72]</a></sup> When he published his <i>Vues des cordillères</i>, he included a color image of the <a href="/wiki/Aztec_calendar_stone" title="Aztec calendar stone">Aztec calendar stone</a>, which had been discovered buried in the <a href="/wiki/Z%C3%B3calo" title="Zócalo">main plaza</a> of Mexico City in 1790, along with select drawings of the <a href="/wiki/Dresden_codex" class="mw-redirect" title="Dresden codex">Dresden codex</a> and others he sought out later in European collections. His aim was to muster evidence that these pictorial and sculptural images could allow the reconstruction of prehispanic history. He sought out Mexican experts in the interpretation of sources from there, especially Antonio Pichardo, who was the literary executor of <a href="/wiki/Antonio_de_Le%C3%B3n_y_Gama" title="Antonio de León y Gama">Antonio de León y Gama</a>'s work. For American-born Spaniards (creoles) who were seeking sources of pride in Mexico's ancient past, Humboldt's recognition of these ancient works and dissemination in his publications was a boon. He read the work of exiled Jesuit <a href="/wiki/Francisco_Javier_Clavijero" title="Francisco Javier Clavijero">Francisco Javier Clavijero</a>, which celebrated Mexico's prehispanic civilization, and which Humboldt invoked to counter the pejorative assertions about the new world by Buffon, de Pauw, and Raynal.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> Humboldt ultimately viewed both the prehispanic realms of Mexico and Peru as despotic and barbaric.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrading1991523-525_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrading1991523-525-74">[74]</a></sup> However, he also drew attention to indigenous monuments and artifacts as cultural productions that had "both ... historical <i>and</i> artistic significance."<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<p>One of his most widely read publications resulting from his travels and investigations in Spanish America was the <i>Essai politique sur le royaum de la Nouvelle Espagne</i>, quickly translated to English as <i>Political Essay on the Kingdom of New Spain</i> (1811).<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> This treatise was the result of Humboldt's own investigations as well as the generosity of Spanish colonial officials for statistical data.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_United_States_1804">The United States 1804</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alexander_von_Humboldt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: The United States 1804">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Louisiana1804a.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Louisiana1804a.jpg/220px-Louisiana1804a.jpg" width="220" height="276" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Louisiana1804a.jpg/330px-Louisiana1804a.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Louisiana1804a.jpg/440px-Louisiana1804a.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2460" data-file-height="3081" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Louisiana1804a.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
1804 Map of the Louisiana Territory. Jefferson and his cabinet sought information from Humboldt when he visited Washington, D.C., about Spain's territory in Mexico, now bordering the U.S.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Leaving from Cuba, Humboldt decided to take an unplanned short visit to the United States. Knowing that the current U.S. president, Thomas Jefferson, was himself a scientist, Humboldt wrote to him saying that he would be in the United States. Jefferson warmly replied, inviting him to visit the White House in the nation's new capital. In his letter Humboldt had gained Jefferson's interest by mentioning that he had discovered mammoth teeth near the Equator. Jefferson had previously written that he believed mammoths had never lived so far south. Humboldt had also hinted at his knowledge of New Spain.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceC_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceC-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<p>Arriving in Philadelphia, which was a center of learning in the U.S., Humboldt met with some of the major scientific figures of the era, including chemist and anatomist <a href="/wiki/Caspar_Wistar_(physician)" title="Caspar Wistar (physician)">Caspar Wistar</a>, who pushed for compulsory smallpox vaccination, and botanist <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Smith_Barton" title="Benjamin Smith Barton">Benjamin Smith Barton</a>, as well as physician <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Rush" title="Benjamin Rush">Benjamin Rush</a>, a signer of the Declaration of Independence, who wished to hear about <a href="/wiki/Cinchona" title="Cinchona">cinchona</a> bark from a South American tree, which cured fevers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDe_Terra1955175-76_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDe_Terra1955175-76-79">[79]</a></sup> Humboldt later published a treatise in English on cinchona.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>After arriving in Washington D.C, Humboldt held numerous intense discussions with Jefferson on both scientific matters and also his year-long stay in New Spain. Jefferson had only recently concluded the Louisiana Purchase, which now placed New Spain on the southwest border of the United States. The Spanish minister in Washington, D.C. had declined to furnish the U.S. government with information about Spanish territories. Humboldt was able to supply Jefferson with the latest information on the population, trade agriculture and military of New Spain. This information would later be the basis for his <i>Essay on the Political Kingdom of New Spain</i> (1810). Jefferson was unsure of where the border of the newly purchased Louisiana was precisely and Humboldt wrote him a two page report on the matter. Jefferson would later refer to Humboldt as "the most scientific man of the age". Albert Gallatin, Secretary of the treasury said of Humboldt, "I was delighted and swallowed more information of various kinds in less than two hours than I had for two years past in all I had read or heard." Gallatin, in turn, supplied Humboldt with information he sought on the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceC_78-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceC-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<p>After six weeks, Humboldt set sail for Europe from the mouth of the <a href="/wiki/Delaware_River" title="Delaware River">Delaware</a> and landed at <a href="/wiki/Bordeaux" title="Bordeaux">Bordeaux</a> on 3 August 1804.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Travel_diaries">Travel diaries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alexander_von_Humboldt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Travel diaries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Humboldt kept a detailed diary of his sojourn to Spanish America, running some 4,000 pages, which he drew on directly for his multiple publications following the expedition. The leather-bound diaries themselves are in now Germany, having been returned from Russia to East Germany, where they were taken by the Red Army after World War II. Following German reunification, the diaries were returned to a descendant of Humboldt. For a time, there was concern about their being sold, but that was averted.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> A government-funded project to digitize the Spanish American expedition as well as his later Russian expedition has been undertaken (2014-2017) by the University of Potsdam and the German State Library - Prussian Cultural Heritage Foundation.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Achievements_of_the_Latin_American_expedition">Achievements of the Latin American expedition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alexander_von_Humboldt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Achievements of the Latin American expedition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Humboldtian_science" title="Humboldtian science">Humboldtian science</a></div>
<p>Humboldt's decades' long endeavor to publish the results of this expedition not only resulted in multiple volumes, but also made his international reputation in scientific circles. Humboldt came to be well-known with the reading public as well, with popular, densely illustrated, condensed versions of his work in multiple languages. Bonpland, his fellow scientist and collaborator on the expedition, collected botanical specimens and preserved them, but unlike Humboldt who had a passion to publish, Bonpland had to be prodded to do the formal descriptions. Many scientific travelers and explorers produced huge visual records, which remained unseen by the general public until the late nineteenth century, in the case of the Malaspina Expedition, and even the late twentieth century, when Mutis's botanical, some 12,000 drawings from New Granada, was published. Humboldt, by contrast, published immediately and continuously, using and ultimately exhausting his personal fortune, to produce both scientific and popular texts. Humboldt's name and fame were made by his travels to Spanish America, particularly his publication of the <i>Political Essay on the Kingdom of New Spain</i>. His image as the premier European scientist was a later development.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
<p>For the Bourbon crown, which had authorized the expedition, the returns were not only tremendous in terms of sheer volume of data on their New World realms, but in dispelling the vague and pejorative assessments of the New World by <a href="/wiki/Guillaume-Thomas_Raynal" class="mw-redirect" title="Guillaume-Thomas Raynal">Guillaume-Thomas Raynal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Georges-Louis_Leclerc,_Comte_de_Buffon" title="Georges-Louis Leclerc, Comte de Buffon">Georges-Louis Leclerc, Comte de Buffon</a>, and <a href="/wiki/William_Robertson_(historian)" title="William Robertson (historian)">William Robertson</a>. The achievements of the Bourbon regime, especially in New Spain, were evident in the precise data Humboldt systematized and published.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrading1991527_63-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrading1991527-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<p>This memorable expedition may be regarded as having laid the foundation of the sciences of <a href="/wiki/Physical_geography" title="Physical geography">physical geography</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plant_geography" class="mw-redirect" title="Plant geography">plant geography</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Meteorology" title="Meteorology">meteorology</a>. Key to that was Humboldt's meticulous and systematic measurement of phenomena with the most advanced instruments then available. He closely observed plant and animal species in situ, not just in isolation, noting all elements in relation to one other. He collected specimens of plants and animals, dividing the growing collection so that if a portion was lost, other parts might survive.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Frederich_heinrich_baron_von_humboldt-charles_wilson_peale.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Frederich_heinrich_baron_von_humboldt-charles_wilson_peale.JPG/170px-Frederich_heinrich_baron_von_humboldt-charles_wilson_peale.JPG" width="170" height="202" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Frederich_heinrich_baron_von_humboldt-charles_wilson_peale.JPG/255px-Frederich_heinrich_baron_von_humboldt-charles_wilson_peale.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Frederich_heinrich_baron_von_humboldt-charles_wilson_peale.JPG/340px-Frederich_heinrich_baron_von_humboldt-charles_wilson_peale.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1735" data-file-height="2065" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Frederich_heinrich_baron_von_humboldt-charles_wilson_peale.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Humboldt depicted by American artist <a href="/wiki/Charles_Willson_Peale" title="Charles Willson Peale">Charles Willson Peale</a>, 1805, who met Humboldt when he visited the U.S. in 1804.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Humboldt saw the need for an approach to science that could account for the harmony of nature among the diversity of the physical world. For Humboldt, "the unity of nature" meant that it was the interrelation of all <a href="/wiki/Physical_sciences" class="mw-redirect" title="Physical sciences">physical sciences</a>—such as the conjoining between <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Meteorology" title="Meteorology">meteorology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Geology" title="Geology">geology</a>—that determined where specific plants grew. He found these relationships by unraveling myriad, painstakingly collected data,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHelferich200425_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHelferich200425-84">[84]</a></sup> data extensive enough that it became an enduring foundation upon which others could base their work. Humboldt viewed nature <a href="/wiki/Holism" title="Holism">holistically</a>, and tried to explain natural phenomena without the appeal to religious dogma. He believed in the central importance of observation, and as a consequence had amassed a vast array of the most sophisticated scientific instruments then available. Each had its own velvet lined box and was the most accurate and portable of its time; nothing quantifiable escaped measurement. According to Humboldt, everything should be measured with the finest and most modern instruments and sophisticated techniques available, for that collected data was the basis of all scientific understanding.</p>
<p>This quantitative methodology would become known as <a href="/wiki/Humboldtian_science" title="Humboldtian science">Humboldtian science</a>. Humboldt wrote "Nature herself is sublimely eloquent. The stars as they sparkle in firmament fill us with delight and ecstasy, and yet they all move in orbit marked out with mathematical precision."<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Zentralbibliothek_Z%C3%BCrich_-_Ideen_zu_einer_Geographie_der_Pflanzen_nebst_einem_Naturgem%C3%A4lde_der_Tropenl%C3%A4nder_-_000012142.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Zentralbibliothek_Z%C3%BCrich_-_Ideen_zu_einer_Geographie_der_Pflanzen_nebst_einem_Naturgem%C3%A4lde_der_Tropenl%C3%A4nder_-_000012142.jpg/220px-Zentralbibliothek_Z%C3%BCrich_-_Ideen_zu_einer_Geographie_der_Pflanzen_nebst_einem_Naturgem%C3%A4lde_der_Tropenl%C3%A4nder_-_000012142.jpg" width="220" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Zentralbibliothek_Z%C3%BCrich_-_Ideen_zu_einer_Geographie_der_Pflanzen_nebst_einem_Naturgem%C3%A4lde_der_Tropenl%C3%A4nder_-_000012142.jpg/330px-Zentralbibliothek_Z%C3%BCrich_-_Ideen_zu_einer_Geographie_der_Pflanzen_nebst_einem_Naturgem%C3%A4lde_der_Tropenl%C3%A4nder_-_000012142.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Zentralbibliothek_Z%C3%BCrich_-_Ideen_zu_einer_Geographie_der_Pflanzen_nebst_einem_Naturgem%C3%A4lde_der_Tropenl%C3%A4nder_-_000012142.jpg/440px-Zentralbibliothek_Z%C3%BCrich_-_Ideen_zu_einer_Geographie_der_Pflanzen_nebst_einem_Naturgem%C3%A4lde_der_Tropenl%C3%A4nder_-_000012142.jpg 2x" data-file-width="15051" data-file-height="10245" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Zentralbibliothek_Z%C3%BCrich_-_Ideen_zu_einer_Geographie_der_Pflanzen_nebst_einem_Naturgem%C3%A4lde_der_Tropenl%C3%A4nder_-_000012142.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Humboldt's <i>Naturgemälde</i>, also known as the Chimborazo Map, is his depiction of the volcano in cross section, with detailed information about plant geography. The illustration was published in <i>The Geography of Plants</i>, 1807, in a large format (54 cm x 84 cm.)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>His <i>Essay on the Geography of Plants</i> (published first in French and then German, both in 1807) was based on the then novel idea of studying the distribution of organic life as affected by varying physical conditions.<sup id="cite_ref-EB1911_14-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EB1911-14">[14]</a></sup> This was most famously depicted in his published cross-section of Chimborazo, approximately two feet by three feet (54&#160;cm x 84&#160;cm) color pictorial, he called <i>Ein Naturgemälde der Anden</i> and what is also called the Chimborazo Map. It was a fold-out at the back of the publication.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> Humboldt first sketched the map when he was in South America, which included written descriptions on either side of the cross-section of Chimborazo. These detailed the information on temperature, altitude, humidity, atmosphere pressure, and the animal and plants (with their scientific names) found at each elevation. Plants from the same genus appear at different elevations. The depiction is on an east-west axis going from the Pacific coast lowlands to the Andean range of which Chimborazo was a part, and the eastern Amazonian basin. Humboldt showed the three zones of coast, mountains, and Amazonia, based on his own observations, but he also drew on existing Spanish sources, particularly <a href="/wiki/Pedro_Cieza_de_Le%C3%B3n" title="Pedro Cieza de León">Pedro Cieza de León</a>, which he explicitly referred to. The Spanish American scientist <a href="/wiki/Francisco_Jos%C3%A9_de_Caldas" title="Francisco José de Caldas">Francisco José de Caldas</a> had also measured and observed mountain environments and had earlier come to similar ideas about environmental factors in the distribution of life forms.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> Humboldt was thus not putting forward something entirely new, but it is argued that his finding is not derivative either.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> The Chimborazo map displayed complex information in an accessible fashion. The map was the basis for comparison with other major peaks. "The Naturgemälde showed for the first time that nature was a global force with corresponding climate zones across continents."<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> Another assessment of the map is that it "marked the beginning of a new era of environmental science, not only of mountain ecology but also of global-scale biogeophysical patterns and processes."<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Woodbridge_isothermal_chart3.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Woodbridge_isothermal_chart3.jpg/220px-Woodbridge_isothermal_chart3.jpg" width="220" height="159" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Woodbridge_isothermal_chart3.jpg/330px-Woodbridge_isothermal_chart3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Woodbridge_isothermal_chart3.jpg/440px-Woodbridge_isothermal_chart3.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3338" data-file-height="2413" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Woodbridge_isothermal_chart3.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Isothermal map of the world using Humboldt's data by <a href="/wiki/William_Channing_Woodbridge" title="William Channing Woodbridge">William Channing Woodbridge</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>By his delineation (in 1817) of <a href="/wiki/Isothermal" class="mw-redirect" title="Isothermal">isothermal</a> lines, he at once suggested the idea and devised the means of comparing the climatic conditions of various countries. He first investigated the rate of decrease in mean temperature with the increase in elevation above sea level, and afforded, by his inquiries regarding the origin of tropical storms, the earliest clue to the detection of the more complicated law governing atmospheric disturbances in higher latitudes.<sup id="cite_ref-EB1911_14-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EB1911-14">[14]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> This was a major contribution to climatology.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDe_Terra1955380_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDe_Terra1955380-92">[92]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
<p>His discovery of the decrease in intensity of Earth's <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic field</a> from the poles to the equator was communicated to the Paris Institute in a memoir read by him on 7 December 1804. Its importance was attested by the speedy emergence of rival claims.<sup id="cite_ref-EB1911_14-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EB1911-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>His services to geology were based on his attentive study of the <a href="/wiki/Volcano" title="Volcano">volcanoes</a> of the Andes and Mexico, which he observed and sketched, climbed, and measured with a variety of instruments. By climbing Chimborazo, he established an altitude record which became the basis for measurement of other volcanoes in the Andes and the Himalayas. As with other aspects of his investigations, he developed methods to show his synthesized results visually, using the graphic method of geologic-cross sections.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDe_Terra1955375-76_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDe_Terra1955375-76-94">[94]</a></sup> He showed that volcanoes fell naturally into linear groups, presumably corresponding with vast subterranean fissures; and by his demonstration of the <a href="/wiki/Igneous_rock" title="Igneous rock">igneous</a> origin of rocks previously held to be of aqueous formation, he contributed largely to the elimination of erroneous views, such as <a href="/wiki/Neptunism" title="Neptunism">Neptunism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-EB1911_14-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EB1911-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>Humboldt was a significant contributor to cartography, creating maps, particularly of New Spain, that became the template for later mapmakers in Mexico. His careful recording of latitude and longitude led to accurate maps of Mexico, the port of Acapulco, the port of Veracruz, and the Valley of Mexico, and a map showing trade patterns among continents. His maps also included schematic information on geography, converting areas of administrative districts (intendancies) using proportional squares.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> The U.S. was keen to see his maps and statistics on New Spain, since they had implication for territorial claims following the Louisiana Purchase.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDe_Terra1955177-78_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDe_Terra1955177-78-96">[96]</a></sup> Later in life, Humboldt published three volumes (1836–39) examining sources that dealt with the early voyages to the Americas, pursuing his interest in nautical astronomy in the fifteenth and sixteenth centuries. His research yielded the origin of the name "America", put on a map of the Americas by <a href="/wiki/Martin_Waldseem%C3%BCller" title="Martin Waldseemüller">Martin Waldseemüller</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Head-claw-Vultur-gryphus-Humboldt-Zoologie-T09p170.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Head-claw-Vultur-gryphus-Humboldt-Zoologie-T09p170.png/220px-Head-claw-Vultur-gryphus-Humboldt-Zoologie-T09p170.png" width="220" height="292" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Head-claw-Vultur-gryphus-Humboldt-Zoologie-T09p170.png/330px-Head-claw-Vultur-gryphus-Humboldt-Zoologie-T09p170.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Head-claw-Vultur-gryphus-Humboldt-Zoologie-T09p170.png/440px-Head-claw-Vultur-gryphus-Humboldt-Zoologie-T09p170.png 2x" data-file-width="1446" data-file-height="1921" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Head-claw-Vultur-gryphus-Humboldt-Zoologie-T09p170.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Humboldt's depiction of a gryphus vulture, an example of his detailed drawing.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Humboldt conducted a census of the indigenous and European inhabitants in <a href="/wiki/New_Spain" title="New Spain">New Spain</a>, publishing a schematized drawing of racial types and populations distribution, grouping them by region and social characteristics.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> He estimated the population to be six million individuals.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> He estimated Indians to be forty percent of New Spain's population, but their distribution being uneven; the most dense were in the center and south of Mexico, the least dense in the north. He presented these data in chart form, for easier understanding.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> He also surveyed the non-Indian population, categorized as Whites (Spaniards), <i>Negroes</i>, and castes (<i><a href="/wiki/Castas" class="mw-redirect" title="Castas">castas</a></i>).<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> American-born Spaniards, so-called <i>creoles</i> had been painting depictions of mixed-race family groupings in the eighteenth century, showing father of one racial category, mother of another, and the offspring in a third category in hierarchical order, so racial hierarchy was an essential way elites viewed Mexican society.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> Although American-born Spaniards were legally racial equals of those born in Spain, crown policy since the Bourbons took the Spanish throne privileged those born in Iberia. Humboldt observed that "the most miserable European, without education and without intellectual cultivation, thinks himself superior to whites born in the new continent."<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> The enmity between the creoles and the peninsular-born whites increasingly became an issue in the late period of Spanish rule, with creoles increasingly alienated from the crown. Humboldt's assessment was that abuses royal government and the example of a new model of rule in the United States were eroded the unity of whites in New Spain.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> Humboldt's writings on race in New Spain were shaped by the memorials of liberal, enlightened Bishop-elect of Michoacán, <a href="/wiki/Manuel_Abad_y_Queipo" title="Manuel Abad y Queipo">Manuel Abad y Queipo</a>, who personally presented Humboldt with his printed memorials to the Spanish crown critiquing social and economic conditions and his recommendations for eliminating them.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup></p>
<p>One scholar says that his writings contain fantastical descriptions of America, while leaving out its inhabitants, stating that Humboldt, coming from the <a href="/wiki/Romanticism" title="Romanticism">Romantic</a> school of thought, believed '...&#160;nature is perfect till man deforms it with care.'<sup id="cite_ref-Pratt_1997_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pratt_1997-107">[107]</a></sup> The further assessment is that he largely neglected the human societies amidst nature. Views of indigenous peoples as 'savage' or 'unimportant' leaves them out of the historical picture.<sup id="cite_ref-Pratt_1997_107-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pratt_1997-107">[107]</a></sup> Other scholars counter that Humboldt dedicated large parts of his work to describing the conditions of slaves, indigenous peoples, mixed-race <a href="/wiki/Castas" class="mw-redirect" title="Castas">castas</a>, and society in general. He often showed his disgust for the slavery<sup id="cite_ref-mcc_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mcc-108">[108]</a></sup> and inhumane conditions in which indigenous peoples and others were treated and he often criticized Spanish colonial policies.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERupke2008138_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERupke2008138-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p>Humboldt was not primarily an artist, but he could draw well, allowing him to record a visual record of particular places and their natural environment. Many of his drawings became the basis for illustrations of his many scientific and general publications. Artists whom Humboldt influenced, such as <a href="/wiki/Johann_Moritz_Rugendas" title="Johann Moritz Rugendas">Johann Moritz Rugendas</a>, followed in his path and painted the same places Humboldt had visited and recorded, such as the basalt formations in Mexico, which was an illustration in his <i>Vues des Cordillères</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup></p>
<p>The editing and publication of the encyclopedic mass of scientific, political and archaeological material that had been collected by him during his absence from Europe was now Humboldt's most urgent desire. After a short trip to Italy with <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Louis_Gay-Lussac" title="Joseph Louis Gay-Lussac">Joseph Louis Gay-Lussac</a> for the purpose of investigating the law of <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_declination" title="Magnetic declination">magnetic declination</a> and a stay of two and a half years in Berlin, in the spring of 1808, he settled in Paris. His purpose for being located there was to secure the scientific cooperation required for bringing his great work through the press. This colossal task, which he at first hoped would occupy but two years, eventually cost him twenty-one, and even then it remained incomplete.</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px">

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Scholarly_and_public_recognition">Scholarly and public recognition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alexander_von_Humboldt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Scholarly and public recognition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Alexander_von_humboldt_1807_225-91-1-PB.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Alexander_von_humboldt_1807_225-91-1-PB.jpeg/170px-Alexander_von_humboldt_1807_225-91-1-PB.jpeg" width="170" height="219" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Alexander_von_humboldt_1807_225-91-1-PB.jpeg/255px-Alexander_von_humboldt_1807_225-91-1-PB.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Alexander_von_humboldt_1807_225-91-1-PB.jpeg/340px-Alexander_von_humboldt_1807_225-91-1-PB.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="1240" data-file-height="1595" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Alexander_von_humboldt_1807_225-91-1-PB.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Humboldt in Berlin 1807</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During his lifetime Humboldt became one of the most famous men in Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESachs20061_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESachs20061-112">[112]</a></sup> Academies, both native and foreign, were eager to elect him to their membership, the first being The <a href="/wiki/American_Philosophical_Society" title="American Philosophical Society">American Philosophical Society</a> in Philadelphia, which he visited at the tail end of his travel through the Americas. He was elected to the <a href="/wiki/Prussian_Academy_of_Sciences" title="Prussian Academy of Sciences">Prussian Academy of Sciences</a> in 1805.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDe_Terra1955204_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDe_Terra1955204-113">[113]</a></sup> Over the years other learned societies in the U.S. elected him a member, including the <a href="/wiki/American_Antiquarian_Society" title="American Antiquarian Society">American Antiquarian Society</a> (Worcester, MA) in 1816;<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Historical_Society" class="mw-redirect" title="New York Historical Society">New York Historical Society</a> in 1820; a Foreign Honorary Member of the <a href="/wiki/American_Academy_of_Arts_and_Sciences" title="American Academy of Arts and Sciences">American Academy of Arts and Sciences</a> in 1822.;<sup id="cite_ref-AAAS_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AAAS-115">[115]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/American_Ethnological_Society" title="American Ethnological Society">American Ethnological Society</a> (New York) in 143; and the American Geographical and Statistical Society, (New York) in 1856.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDe_Terra1955377_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDe_Terra1955377-116">[116]</a></sup> He was elected a foreign member of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Swedish_Academy_of_Sciences" title="Royal Swedish Academy of Sciences">Royal Swedish Academy of Sciences</a> in 1810. The <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">Royal Society</a>, whose president Sir Joseph Banks had aided Humboldt as a young man, now welcomed him as a foreign member.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWulf2015166_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWulf2015166-117">[117]</a></sup></p>
<p>After Mexican independence from Spain in 1821, the Mexican government recognized him with high honors for his services to the nation. In 1827, the first <a href="/wiki/President_of_Mexico" title="President of Mexico">President of Mexico</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guadalupe_Victoria" title="Guadalupe Victoria">Guadalupe Victoria</a> granted Humboldt Mexican citizenship<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> and in 1859, the President of Mexico, <a href="/wiki/Benito_Ju%C3%A1rez" title="Benito Juárez">Benito Juárez</a>, named Humboldt a hero of the nation (<i>benemérito de la nación</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> The gestures were purely honorary; he never returned to the Americas following his expedition.</p>
<p>Importantly for Humboldt's long term financial stability, King <a href="/wiki/Frederick_William_III_of_Prussia" title="Frederick William III of Prussia">Frederick William III of Prussia</a> conferred upon him the honor of the post of royal chamberlain, without at the time exacting the duties. The appointment had a pension of 2,500 <a href="/wiki/Thaler" title="Thaler">thalers</a>, afterwards doubled. This official stipend became his main source of income in later years when he exhausted his fortune on the publications of his research. Financial necessity forced his permanent relocation to Berlin in 1827 from Paris. In Paris he found not only scientific sympathy, but the social stimulus which his vigorous and healthy mind eagerly craved. He was equally in his element as the lion of the <a href="/wiki/Salon_(gathering)" title="Salon (gathering)">salons</a> and as the savant of the <a href="/wiki/Institut_de_France" title="Institut de France">Institut de France</a> and the observatory.</p>
<p>On 12 May 1827, he settled permanently in Berlin, where his first efforts were directed towards the furtherance of the science of terrestrial magnetism. In 1827, he began giving public lectures in Berlin, which became the basis for his last major publication, <a href="/wiki/Kosmos_(Humboldt)" title="Kosmos (Humboldt)"><i>Kosmos</i></a> (1845–62).<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup></p>
<p>For many years, it had been one of his favorite schemes to secure, by means of simultaneous observations at distant points, a thorough investigation of the nature and law of "<a href="/wiki/Magnetic_storm" class="mw-redirect" title="Magnetic storm">magnetic storms</a>" (a term invented by him to designate abnormal disturbances of <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_magnetic_field" title="Earth's magnetic field">Earth's magnetism</a>). The meeting at Berlin, on 18 September 1828, of a newly formed scientific association, of which he was elected president, gave him the opportunity of setting on foot an extensive system of research in combination with his diligent personal observations. His appeal to the Russian government, in 1829, led to the establishment of a line of magnetic and meteorological stations across northern Asia. Meanwhile, his letter to the <a href="/wiki/Prince_Augustus_Frederick,_Duke_of_Sussex" title="Prince Augustus Frederick, Duke of Sussex">Duke of Sussex</a>, then (April 1836) president of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">Royal Society</a>, secured for the undertaking, the wide basis of the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British dominions</a>.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Encyclop%C3%A6dia_Britannica_Eleventh_Edition" title="Encyclopædia Britannica Eleventh Edition"><i>Encyclopædia Britannica</i>, Eleventh Edition</a>, observes, "Thus that scientific conspiracy of nations which is one of the noblest fruits of modern civilization was by his exertions first successfully organized".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClerke1911874_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClerke1911874-121">[121]</a></sup> However, earlier examples of international scientific cooperation exist, notably the 18th-century observations of the <a href="/wiki/Transit_of_Venus" title="Transit of Venus">transits of Venus</a>.</p>
<p>In 1869, the 100th year of his birth, Humboldt's fame was so great that cities all over America celebrated his birth with large festivals. In New York City, a bust of his head was unveiled in Central Park.<sup id="cite_ref-Nichols.2C_Sandra_2006_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nichols.2C_Sandra_2006-122">[122]</a></sup></p>
<p>Scholars have speculated about the reasons for Humboldt's declining renown among the public. Sandra Nichols has argued that there are three reasons for this. First, a trend towards specialization in scholarship. Humboldt was a generalist who connected many disciplines in his work. Today, academics have become more and more focused on narrow fields of work. Humboldt combined <a href="/wiki/Ecology" title="Ecology">ecology</a>, geography and even social sciences. Second, a change in writing style. Humboldt's works, which were considered essential to a library in 1869, had flowery prose that fell out of fashion. One critic said they had a "laborious picturesqueness". Humboldt himself said that, "If I only knew how to describe adequately how and what I felt, I might, after this long journey of mine, really be able to give happiness to people. The disjointed life I lead makes me hardly certain of my way of writing". Third, a rising anti-German sentiment in the late 1800s and the early 1900s due to heavy German immigration to the United States and later World War 1.<sup id="cite_ref-Nichols.2C_Sandra_2006_122-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nichols.2C_Sandra_2006-122">[122]</a></sup> On the eve of the 1959 hundredth anniversary of the death of Humboldt, the government of West Germany planned significant celebrations in conjunction with nations that Humboldt visited.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Expedition_in_Russia.2C_1829">Expedition in Russia, 1829</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alexander_von_Humboldt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Expedition in Russia, 1829">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pickersgill_humboldt.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Pickersgill_humboldt.jpg/220px-Pickersgill_humboldt.jpg" width="220" height="284" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Pickersgill_humboldt.jpg/330px-Pickersgill_humboldt.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Pickersgill_humboldt.jpg/440px-Pickersgill_humboldt.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2310" data-file-height="2982" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Pickersgill_humboldt.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Alexander von Humboldt, portrait by W. Pickersgill (1831)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1811, and again in 1818, projects of Asiatic exploration were proposed to Humboldt, first by Czar <a href="/wiki/Nicolas_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Nicolas I">Nicolas I</a>'s Russian government, and afterwards by the Prussian government; but on each occasion, untoward circumstances interposed. It was not until he had begun his sixtieth year that he resumed his early role of traveler in the interests of science.</p>
<p>The Russian Foreign Minister, Count <a href="/wiki/Georg_von_Cancrin" title="Georg von Cancrin">Georg von Cancrin</a> contacted Humboldt about whether a platinum-based currency was possible in Russia and invited him to visit the Ural Mountains. Humboldt was not encouraging about a <a href="/wiki/Platinum" title="Platinum">platinum</a>-based currency, when silver was the standard as a world currency. But the invitation to visit the Urals was intriguing, especially since Humboldt had long dreamed of going to Asia. He had wanted to travel to India and made considerable efforts to persuade the <a href="/wiki/British_East_India_Company" class="mw-redirect" title="British East India Company">British East India Company</a> to authorize a trip, but those efforts were fruitless.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWulf2015171-74.2C_199-200_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWulf2015171-74.2C_199-200-124">[124]</a></sup> When Russia renewed its earlier invitation to Humboldt, Humboldt accepted.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWulf2015199-200_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWulf2015199-200-125">[125]</a></sup> The Russians sought to entice Humboldt by engaging his enduring interest in mining sites, for comparative scientific purposes for Humboldt, but for the Russians to gain expert knowledge about their resources. For Humboldt, the Russian monarch's promise to fund the trip was extremely important, since Humboldt's inherited 100,000 thaler fortune was gone and he lived on the Prussian government pension of 2,500-3,000 thalers as the monarch's chamberlain. The Russian government gave an advance of 1200 <i>chervontsev</i> in Berlin and another 20,000 when he arrived in St Petersburg.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup></p>
<p>Humboldt was eager to travel not just to the Urals, but also across the steppes of Siberia to Russia's border with China. Humboldt wrote Cancrin saying that he intended to learn Russian to read mining journals in the language.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDe_Terra1955283-85_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDe_Terra1955283-85-127">[127]</a></sup> As the details of the expedition were worked out, Humboldt said that he would travel to Russia in his own French coach, with a German servant, as well as <a href="/wiki/Gustav_Rose" title="Gustav Rose">Gustav Rose</a>, a professor of chemistry and mineralogy. He also invited <a href="/wiki/Christian_Gottfried_Ehrenberg" title="Christian Gottfried Ehrenberg">Christian Gottfried Ehrenberg</a> to join the expedition, to study water micro-organisms in <a href="/wiki/Lake_Baikal" title="Lake Baikal">Lake Baikal</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Caspian_Sea" title="Caspian Sea">Caspian Sea</a>. Humboldt himself was keen to continue his studies of magnetism of mountains and mineral deposits. As was usual for his research, he brought scientific instruments to take the most accurate measurements.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDe_Terra1955287_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDe_Terra1955287-128">[128]</a></sup> The Russians organized the local arrangements, including lodging, horses, accompanying crew. Humboldt's title for the expedition was as an official of the Department of Mines. As the expedition neared dangerous areas, he had to travel in a convoy with an escort.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup></p>
<p>Physically Humboldt was in good condition, despite his advancing years, writing to Cancrin "I still walk very lightly on foot, nine to ten hours without resting, despite my age and my white hair."<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup></p>
<p>Between May and November 1829, he and the growing expedition traversed the wide expanse of the Russian empire from the Neva to the Yenesei, accomplishing in twenty-five weeks a distance of 9,614 miles (15,472&#160;km). Humboldt and the expedition party traveled by coach on well maintained roads, with rapid progress being made because of changes of horses at way stations. The party had grown, with Johann Seifert, who was a huntsman and collector of animal specimens; a Russian mining official; Count Adolphe Polier, one of Humboldt's friends from Paris; a cook; plus a contingent of <a href="/wiki/Cossacks" title="Cossacks">Cossacks</a> for security. Three carriages were filled with people, supplies, and scientific instruments. For Humboldt's magnetic readings to be accurate, they carried an iron-free tent.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWulf2015201-202_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWulf2015201-202-131">[131]</a></sup> This expedition was unlike his Spanish American travels with Bonpland, with the two alone and sometimes accompanied by local guides.</p>
<p>The Russian government was interested in Humboldt finding prospects for mining and commercial advancement of the realm and made it clear that Humboldt was not to investigate social issues, nor criticize social conditions of Russian <a href="/wiki/Serfs" class="mw-redirect" title="Serfs">serfs</a>. In his publications on Spanish America, he did comment on the conditions of the indigenous populations, and deplored black slavery, but well after he had left those territories.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWulf2015205_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWulf2015205-132">[132]</a></sup> As Humboldt discovered, the government kept tight control of the expedition, even when it was 1,000 miles (1,600&#160;km) from Moscow, with local government officials greeting the expedition at every stop. The itinerary was planned with Tobolsk the farthest destination, then a return to St Petersburg.</p>
<p>Humboldt wrote to the Russian Minister Cancrin that he was extending his travel, knowing that the missive would not reach him in time to scuttle the plan. The further east he journeyed into wilder territory, the more Humboldt enjoyed it. They still followed the Siberian Highway and made excellent progress, sometimes a hundred miles (160&#160;km) in a day.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWulf2015206-207_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWulf2015206-207-133">[133]</a></sup> Although they were halted at the end of July and warned of an <a href="/wiki/Anthrax" title="Anthrax">anthrax</a> outbreak, Humboldt decided to continue despite the danger. "At my age, nothing should be postponed."<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup></p>
<p>The journey though carried out with all the advantages afforded by the immediate patronage of the Russian government, was too rapid to be profitable scientifically. The correction of the prevalent exaggerated estimate of the height of the Central Asian plateau, and the prediction of the discovery of diamonds in the gold-washings of the Urals, were important aspects of these travels. In the end, the expedition took 8 months, traveled 15,500&#160;km, stopped at 658 post stations, and used 12,244 horses.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup></p>
<p>One writer claims that "Nothing was quite as Humboldt wanted it. The entire expedition was a compromise.".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWulf2015203_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWulf2015203-136">[136]</a></sup> The Russian emperor offered Humboldt an invitation to return to Russia, but Humboldt declined, due to his disapproval of Nicholas's restrictions on his freedom of movement during the expedition and his ability to freely report on it.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDe_Terra1955306_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDe_Terra1955306-137">[137]</a></sup> Humboldt published two works on the Russian expedition, first <i>Fragmens de géologie et de la climatologie asiastique</i> in 1831, based on lectures he gave on the topic. In 1843, he completed the three-volume <i>Asie Centrale</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> which he dedicated to Czar Nicholas, which he called "an unavoidable step, as the expedition was accomplished at his expense."<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> As of 2016, these works have not been translated to English.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWulf2015433_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWulf2015433-140">[140]</a></sup> His 1829 expedition to Russia when he was an old man is much less known than his five-year travels in Spanish America, which had resulted in many published volumes over the decades since his 1804 return. Nevertheless, it gave Humboldt comparative data for his various later scientific publications.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cosmos"><i>Cosmos</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alexander_von_Humboldt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Cosmos">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Alexander_von_Humboldt&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. </table>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Alexander_von_Humboldt_photo_1857.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Alexander_von_Humboldt_photo_1857.jpg/170px-Alexander_von_Humboldt_photo_1857.jpg" width="170" height="206" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Alexander_von_Humboldt_photo_1857.jpg/255px-Alexander_von_Humboldt_photo_1857.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Alexander_von_Humboldt_photo_1857.jpg/340px-Alexander_von_Humboldt_photo_1857.jpg 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="607" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Alexander_von_Humboldt_photo_1857.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Photograph of Humboldt in his later years</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Kosmos_(Humboldt)" title="Kosmos (Humboldt)"><i>Kosmos</i></a> was Humboldt's multi-volume effort in his later years to write a work bringing together all the research from his long career. The writing took shape in <a href="/wiki/Public_lecture" title="Public lecture">lectures</a> he delivered before the <a href="/wiki/Humboldt_University_of_Berlin" title="Humboldt University of Berlin">University of Berlin</a> in the winter of 1827–28. These lectures would form "the <a href="/wiki/Cartoon" title="Cartoon">cartoon</a> for the great fresco of the <i>[K]osmos</i>".<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> His 1829 expedition to Russia supplied him with data comparative to his Latin American expedition.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWulf2015235_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWulf2015235-142">[142]</a></sup></p>
<p>The first two volumes of the <i>Kosmos</i> were published between the years 1845 and 1847 were intended to comprise the entire work, but Humboldt published three more volumes, one of which was posthumous. Humboldt had long aimed to write a comprehensive work about geography and the natural sciences. The work attempted to unify the sciences then known in a Kantian framework. With inspiration from <a href="/wiki/German_Romanticism" title="German Romanticism">German Romanticism</a>, Humboldt sought to create a compendium of the world's environment.<sup id="cite_ref-Walls_2009_3.E2.80.9315_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Walls_2009_3.E2.80.9315-10">[10]</a></sup> He spent the last decade of his long life — as he called them, his "improbable" years — continuing this work. The third and fourth volumes were published in 1850–58; a fragment of a fifth appeared posthumously in 1862.</p>
<p>His reputation had long since been made with his publications on the Latin American expedition. There is not a consensus on the importance of <i>Kosmos</i>. One scholar, who stresses the importance of Humboldt's <i>Political Essay on the Kingdom of New Spain</i> as essential reading, dismisses <i>Kosmos</i> as "little more than an academic curiosity."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrading1991534_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrading1991534-143">[143]</a></sup> A different opinion is that <i>Kosmos</i> was his "most influential book."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWulf2015235_142-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWulf2015235-142">[142]</a></sup></p>
<p>As with most of Humboldt's works, <i>Kosmos</i> was also translated into multiple languages in editions of uneven quality. It was very popular in Britain and America. In 1849 a German newspaper commented that in England two of the three different translations were made by women, "while in Germany most of the men do not understand it."<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> The first translation by Augustin Pritchard — published anonymously by Mr. Baillière (volume I in 1845 and volume II in 1848) — suffered from being hurriedly made. In a letter Humboldt said of it: "It will damage my reputation. All the charm of my description is destroyed by an English sounding like Sanskrit."<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>The other two translations were made by Mrs. Sabine under the superintendence of her husband Col. <a href="/wiki/Edward_Sabine" title="Edward Sabine">Edward Sabine</a> (4 volumes 1846–1858), and by Miss E.C. Otté (5 volumes 1849–1858, the only complete translation of the 4 German volumes). These three translations were also published in the United States. The numbering of the volumes differs between the German and the English editions. Volume 3 of the German edition corresponds to the volumes 3 and 4 of the English translation, as the German volume appeared in 2 parts in 1850 and 1851. Volume 5 of the German edition was not translated until 1981, again by a woman.<sup id="cite_ref-Bowen1981_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bowen1981-145">[145]</a></sup> Miss Otté's translation benefited from a detailed table of contents, and an index for every volume; of the German edition only volumes 4 and 5 had (extremely short) tables of contents, and the index to the whole work only appeared with volume 5 in 1862. Less well known in Germany is the atlas belonging to the German edition of the Cosmos <i>"Berghaus' Physikalischer Atlas"</i>, better known as the pirated version by Traugott Bromme under the title <i>"Atlas zu Alexander von Humboldt's Kosmos"</i> (Stuttgart 1861).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>In Britain, <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Berghaus" title="Heinrich Berghaus">Heinrich Berghaus</a> planned to publish together with <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Keith_Johnston_(1804-1871)" class="mw-redirect" title="Alexander Keith Johnston (1804-1871)">Alexander Keith Johnston</a> a <i>"Physical Atlas"</i>. But later Johnston published it alone under the title <i>"The Physical Atlas of Natural Phenomena"</i>. In Britain its connection to the <i>Cosmos</i> seems not have been recognized.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Influence_on_scientists_and_artists">Influence on scientists and artists</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alexander_von_Humboldt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Influence on scientists and artists">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Humboldt was generous toward his friends and mentored young scientists. He and Bonpland parted ways after their return to Europe, and Humboldt largely took on the task of publishing the results of their Latin American expedition at Humboldt's expense, but he included Bonpland as co-author on the nearly published 30 volumes. Bonpland returned to Latin America, settling in Buenos Aires, Argentina, then moved to the countryside near the border with Paraguay. The forces of Dr. <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Gaspar_Rodr%C3%ADguez_de_Francia" title="José Gaspar Rodríguez de Francia">José Gaspar Rodríguez de Francia</a>, the strong man of Paraguay, abducted Bonpland after killing Bonpland's estate workers. Bonpland was accused of "agricultural espionage" and of threatening Paraguay's virtual monopoly on the cultivation of <a href="/wiki/Yerba_mate" title="Yerba mate">yerba mate</a>. Despite international pressure, including the British government and Simón Bolívar's, along with European scientists including Humboldt, Francia kept Bonpland prisoner until 1831. He was released after nearly 10 years in Paraguay. Humboldt and Bonpland maintained a warm correspondence about science and politics until Bonpland's death in 1858.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWulf2015272_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWulf2015272-147">[147]</a></sup></p>
<p>During Humboldt's time in Paris, he met in 1818 the young and brilliant Peruvian student of the Royal Mining School of Paris, <a href="/wiki/Mariano_Eduardo_de_Rivero_y_Ustariz" title="Mariano Eduardo de Rivero y Ustariz">Mariano Eduardo de Rivero y Ustariz</a>. Subsequently Humboldt acted as a mentor of the career of this promising Peruvian scientist. Another recipient of Humboldt's aid was <a href="/wiki/Louis_Agassiz" title="Louis Agassiz">Louis Agassiz</a> (1807–1873) was directly aided with needed cash from Humboldt, assistance in securing an academic position, and help with getting his research on zoology published. Agassiz sent him copies of his publications and went on to gain considerable scientific recognition as a professor at Harvard.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDe_Terra1955326-27_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDe_Terra1955326-27-148">[148]</a></sup> Agassiz delivered an address to the Boston Society of Natural History in 1869, on the centenary of his patron's birth.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> When Humboldt was an elderly man, he aided another young scholar, <a href="/wiki/Gotthold_Eisenstein" title="Gotthold Eisenstein">Gotthold Eisenstein</a>, a brilliant, young, Jewish mathematician in Berlin, for whom he obtained a small crown pension and nominated for the Academy of Science.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDe_Terra1955334-36_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDe_Terra1955334-36-150">[150]</a></sup></p>
<p>Humboldt's popular writings inspired many scientists and naturalists, including <a href="/wiki/Charles_Darwin" title="Charles Darwin">Charles Darwin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henry_David_Thoreau" title="Henry David Thoreau">Henry David Thoreau</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Muir" title="John Muir">John Muir</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_Perkins_Marsh" title="George Perkins Marsh">George Perkins Marsh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Haeckel" title="Ernst Haeckel">Ernst Haeckel</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> as well as brothers <a href="/wiki/Richard_Schomburgk" class="mw-redirect" title="Richard Schomburgk">Richard Schomburgk</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Schomburgk" class="mw-redirect" title="Robert Schomburgk">Robert Schomburgk</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup></p>
<p>Humboldt carried on correspondence with many and two volumes of letters to <a href="/wiki/Karl_August_Varnhagen_von_Ense" title="Karl August Varnhagen von Ense">Karl August Varnhagen von Ense</a> have been published.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup></p>
<p>Charles Darwin made frequent reference to Humboldt's work in his <i><a href="/wiki/The_Voyage_of_the_Beagle" title="The Voyage of the Beagle">Voyage of the Beagle</a></i>, where Darwin described his own scientific exploration of the Americas. In one note, he placed Humboldt first on the "list of American travellers".<sup id="cite_ref-Darjournal_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Darjournal-155">[155]</a></sup> Darwin's work was influenced by Humboldt's writing style as well. Darwin's sister remarked to him "you had, probably from reading so much of Humboldt, got his phraseology and the kind of flower French expressions he uses."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWulf2015226_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWulf2015226-156">[156]</a></sup></p>
<p>When this <i>Journal</i> was published, Darwin sent a copy to Humboldt, who responded "You told me in your kind letter that, when you were young, the manner in which I studied and depicted nature in the torrid zones contributed toward exciting in you the ardour and desire to travel in distant lands. Considering the importance of your work, Sir, this may be the greatest success that my humble work could bring."(Needs ref) In his autobiography, Darwin recalled reading "with care and profound interest Humboldt's <i>Personal Narrative</i>" and finding it one of the two most influential books on his work, which had "stirred up in me a burning zeal to add even the most humble contribution to the noble structure of Natural Science."<sup id="cite_ref-Darbio_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Darbio-157">[157]</a></sup></p>
<p>Humboldt would later reveal to Darwin in the 1840s that he had been a fan of Darwin's grandfather's poetry. Erasmus Darwin had published the poem <i>Loves of the Plants</i> in the early 1800s. Humboldt praised the poem for combing nature and imagination, a theme that permeated Humboldt's own work.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWulf201537_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWulf201537-158">[158]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Church_Heart_of_the_Andes.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Church_Heart_of_the_Andes.jpg/220px-Church_Heart_of_the_Andes.jpg" width="220" height="121" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Church_Heart_of_the_Andes.jpg/330px-Church_Heart_of_the_Andes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Church_Heart_of_the_Andes.jpg/440px-Church_Heart_of_the_Andes.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3787" data-file-height="2080" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Church_Heart_of_the_Andes.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Frederic_Edwin_Church" title="Frederic Edwin Church">Frederic Edwin Church</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Heart_of_the_Andes" title="The Heart of the Andes">The Heart of the Andes</a> (1859).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A number of nineteenth-century artists traveled to Latin America, following in the footsteps of Humboldt, painting landscapes and scenes of everyday life. <a href="/wiki/Johann_Moritz_Rugendas" title="Johann Moritz Rugendas">Johann Moritz Rugendas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_Bellermann" title="Ferdinand Bellermann">Ferdinand Bellermann</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Eduard_Hildebrandt" title="Eduard Hildebrandt">Eduard Hildebrandt</a> were three important European painters.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Frederic_Edwin_Church" title="Frederic Edwin Church">Frederic Edwin Church</a> was the most famous landscape painter in the U.S. in the nineteenth century. His paintings of Andean volcanoes that Humboldt climbed helped make Church's reputation. His 5 foot by 10 foot painting entitled <a href="/wiki/Heart_of_the_Andes" class="mw-redirect" title="Heart of the Andes">Heart of the Andes</a> "caused a sensation" when it was completed. Church had hoped to ship the painting to Berlin to show the painting to Humboldt, but Humboldt died a few days after Church's letter was written.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup> Church painted Cotopaxi three times, twice in 1855 and then in 1859 in eruption.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/George_Catlin" title="George Catlin">George Catlin</a>, most famous for his portraits of North American Indians and paintings of life among various North American tribes also traveled to South America, producing a number of paintings. He wrote to Humboldt in 1855, sending him his proposal for South American travels. Humboldt replied, thanking him and sending a memorandum helping guide his travels.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup></p>
